  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d8380e39-135b-4844-a15d-01279010df9f'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14529353506913783917">Humanity pushed its way to the stars - and encountered the Gbaba, a ruthless alien race that nearly wiped us out.<br/><br/>Earth and her colonies are now smoldering ruins, and the few survivors have fled to distant, Earth-like Safehold, to try to rebuild. But the Gbaba can detect the emissions of an industrial civilization, so the human rulers of Safehold have taken extraordinary me</span>
  <span id="freeText14529353506913783917" style="display:none">Humanity pushed its way to the stars - and encountered the Gbaba, a ruthless alien race that nearly wiped us out.<br /><br />Earth and her colonies are now smoldering ruins, and the few survivors have fled to distant, Earth-like Safehold, to try to rebuild. But the Gbaba can detect the emissions of an industrial civilization, so the human rulers of Safehold have taken extraordinary measures: with mind control and hidden high technology, they've built a religion in which every Safeholdian believes, a religion designed to keep Safehold society medieval forever.<br /><br />800 years pass. In a hidden chamber on Safehold, an android from the far human past awakens. This "rebirth" was set in motion centuries before, by a faction that opposed shackling humanity with a concocted religion. Via automated recordings, "Nimue" - or, rather, the android with the memories of Lieutenant Commander Nimue Alban - is told her fate: she will emerge into Safeholdian society, suitably disguised, and begin the process of provoking the technological progress which the Church of God Awaiting has worked for centuries to prevent.<br /><br />Nothing about this will be easy. To better deal with a medieval society, "Nimue" takes a new gender and a new name, "Merlin." His formidable powers and access to caches of hidden high technology will need to be carefully concealed. And he'll need to find a base of operations, a Safeholdian country that's just a little more freewheeling, a little less orthodox, a little more open to the new.<br /><br />And thus Merlin comes to Charis, a mid-sized kingdom with a talent for naval warfare. He plans to make the acquaintance of King Haarahld and Crown Prince Cayleb, and maybe, just maybe, kick off a new era of invention. Which is bound to draw the attention of the Church...and, inevitably, lead to war.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14529353506913783917" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/800255-does-anyone-know-why-there-are-more-books">Does anyone know why there are more books in the German language version of the series than there are in the English series?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_800255'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F72199.Off_Armageddon_Reef">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/800255-does-anyone-know-why-there-are-more-books">4 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/800255-does-anyone-know-why-there-are-more-books">Add your answer</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/28572160-nathan"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1392069441i/28572160._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/28572160-nathan">Nathan</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Maybe you already found your answer, but this is because German is a longer language, with longer words and longer sentences than English. Thus, to pr<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Maybe you already found your answer, but this is because German is a longer language, with longer words and longer sentences than English. Thus, to prevent the books being too big, the story is split up into multiple books. GRRM's A Song of Ice and Fire series is up to 10 books in German, but it's still the same amount of story as in the 5 English books.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer296059601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17181905439686030963">For imagination and scope of vision this must have high praise. <br/><br/>For an entertaining and thought provoking blend of science fiction and fantasy, this also gets high praise. <br/><br/>But ... <br/><br/>It is very long, tends towards space opera melodrama and somewhat collapses under its own weight. It was also formulaic and predictable, though in fairness to Weber, he fills in the predictable gaps with great attention to detail. Some of the dialogue was flat, but most of the narrative was OK to read. <br/><br/> Just … too much</span>
  <span id="freeText17181905439686030963" style="display:none">For imagination and scope of vision this must have high praise. <br><br>For an entertaining and thought provoking blend of science fiction and fantasy, this also gets high praise. <br><br>But ... <br><br>It is very long, tends towards space opera melodrama and somewhat collapses under its own weight. It was also formulaic and predictable, though in fairness to Weber, he fills in the predictable gaps with great attention to detail. Some of the dialogue was flat, but most of the narrative was OK to read. <br><br> Just … too much, kind of like the feeling you have after eating at an all you can eat discount buffet.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433807647i/15145573.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2244594396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14324193314023843965">Swashbuckling read that dragged on a bit at times. <br/><br/>Don't get me wrong. I liked it, but could have trimmed a bit or maybe I'm just a whiner because it spent more time on the conflict of secular and spiritual forces and less on boats. More boats!<br/><br/>This basically reinterprets the Age of Sail and how innovation drives economy, growth and change. Not everyone likes change, but it is a result of innovation. New ideas, new solutions cause one to question fundamental principles. Either one embraces it or </span>
  <span id="freeText14324193314023843965" style="display:none">Swashbuckling read that dragged on a bit at times. <br /><br />Don't get me wrong. I liked it, but could have trimmed a bit or maybe I'm just a whiner because it spent more time on the conflict of secular and spiritual forces and less on boats. More boats!<br /><br />This basically reinterprets the Age of Sail and how innovation drives economy, growth and change. Not everyone likes change, but it is a result of innovation. New ideas, new solutions cause one to question fundamental principles. Either one embraces it or not, and the ensuing battle goes from metaphorical to physical faster than you think. <br /><br />At the end of the day, a civilization either moves with the tide or gets drowned by it. Stagnation is death, just slow. I guess the blatant manipulation in this book is amusing/ironic/slightly terrifying when you think of present day consequences, but in establishing the world, Webber cribbed it straight out of history-- 15th and 16th century. If you don't know it, then it's probably necessary, but if you have a grasp of it then you spend your time saying, "Come on, come on--Let's go!"<br /><br />Then there's the tedious repetitions as multi-players are pulled into a scheme and we have to hear how each one reacts to the same event and I think it could have been tightened. Second thing that really made me roll my eyes, not aggravate, more quirked brow, was the spellings of names. I love sci-fi fantasy; I get it. I'm all for weird. But taking essentially the same names and just replacing all or the majority of vowels with "Y"s was... lame. <br /><br />On the other hand, love the references to Hamilcar and Hasdrubal. Yeah, I read that Hannibal book last year so this passing/throwaway nod was nice. There's a bunch, some dorkier than others, but fun.<br /><br />If you've read enough reviews of mine then you've probably noticed a slight fixation on boats, sailing, islands, etc. and this was a big draw for the book. And I was NOT disappointed. Innovations in boat design as well as a good eye for naval military maneuvers were stellar. Seriously, the naval warfare and all the intensity and horrific consequences, the best two hundred pages of the book. As much as this dragged on, I'll give the sequel a go, because Duh... BOATS and I like the main characters.<br /><br /><u>To sum up in LA Style:</u> this is Henry VIII meets Pern on a bed of Borg and Lucifer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14324193314023843965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146435732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8359002124201806783">Great premise, but mostly unreadable. I guess the editor thought so, too, because I lost count of the typos, repeated words, and grammatical mistakes. Name spellings were a silly distraction throughout the entire book.<br/><br/>Even without the cosmetic errors, however, this book is a slog. The pacing is uneven, and ironically slows to a crawl whenever "intrigue"—I use the word advisedly—is afoot, as the characters discuss every nuance of every potential course of action. There's really not much for the r</span>
  <span id="freeText8359002124201806783" style="display:none">Great premise, but mostly unreadable. I guess the editor thought so, too, because I lost count of the typos, repeated words, and grammatical mistakes. Name spellings were a silly distraction throughout the entire book.<br /><br />Even without the cosmetic errors, however, this book is a slog. The pacing is uneven, and ironically slows to a crawl whenever "intrigue"—I use the word advisedly—is afoot, as the characters discuss every nuance of every potential course of action. There's really not much for the reader to do except let all this didactic dialogue wash over him, because for all the political maneuvering and extraneous characters, the story is utterly predictable.<br /><br />Weber stops explaining every little thing only after the story turns into the naval epic it always wanted to be (yeah, that happened). Suddenly, the reader must be an expert on 18th-century sailing terms, since there's no time now for even the briefest explanation. At least there's a return to action, however, and stuff actually <em>happens.</em><br /><br />If you must, read the first 200 pages and the last 100. The 500 in the middle add nothing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8359002124201806783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17707301587180442029">David Weber ... oh my. David Weber has a horrible, bad habit of going on and on and on and on and on and on and on -- and on and on some more -- in this book about things that could simply be edited and still leave the story intact. Quite aside from the fact that, reading about him going on and on and on about muzzle-loading cannons made me wonder where I'd read it before, and then I realized it was in the Honor Harrington series, about the multi-drive missiles. I read this book to the end but i</span>
  <span id="freeText17707301587180442029" style="display:none">David Weber ... oh my. David Weber has a horrible, bad habit of going on and on and on and on and on and on and on -- and on and on some more -- in this book about things that could simply be edited and still leave the story intact. Quite aside from the fact that, reading about him going on and on and on about muzzle-loading cannons made me wonder where I'd read it before, and then I realized it was in the Honor Harrington series, about the multi-drive missiles. I read this book to the end but it was exhausting and unrewarding, and what I dislike about that is that Weber is a good writer who evidently has lost control of his pen. He needs a good editor to keep him in line, and it doesn't appear that he's getting one.</span>

<div id="review_39912961" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39912961" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1177534-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593697601p2/1177534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39912961?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/1177534-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=obscure-references-book-group">obscure-references-book-group</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1177534-michael?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39912961" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8602862997965182527">The prologue portion of "Off Armageddon Reef" is a fascinating, compelling and interesting set-up for what could have been a great book.<br/><br/>Humanity is facing extermination at the hands of a ruthless enemy. The last remnants of a fleet are making a last stand in space in an attempt to hide a colony effort that will remove technology from humanity and, hopefully, render the colony off the aliens' radar.<br/><br/>The plan succeeds with some fancy tricks, but then it's revealed there are schisms within the human</span>
  <span id="freeText8602862997965182527" style="display:none">The prologue portion of "Off Armageddon Reef" is a fascinating, compelling and interesting set-up for what could have been a great book.<br /><br />Humanity is facing extermination at the hands of a ruthless enemy. The last remnants of a fleet are making a last stand in space in an attempt to hide a colony effort that will remove technology from humanity and, hopefully, render the colony off the aliens' radar.<br /><br />The plan succeeds with some fancy tricks, but then it's revealed there are schisms within the human fleet on how take our technology level back in time and make sure we stay there. <br /><br />It's at this point the novel takes a left turn for the next 500 or so pages, looking at the colony world. The story goes from epic sci-fi to a fantasy novel with a few elements and questions about the nature of belief and religion thrown in for good measure.<br /><br />The problem is that in the tonal shift, the narrative slows down to the point that watching paint dry would be faster. Weber seems to be in a need of a good edit or two to trim out some of the fat of this novel (which I hear this series could go as far as eight books...) and maybe the series as a whole. There are some naval battles that you can tell Weber is having a good time writing, but the book as a whole ends with a sigh rather than on a rousing note. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8602862997965182527" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16915533430333436512" style="display:none">Think of this as Commander Data at Trafalgar. The science fiction framing story rarely impinges on the sixteenth century technology of the main story, except in the form of a superhuman android who, not surprisingly, turns everything upside down. The "how" and "why" are well told, but the premise is so weak that it flunks the logic of the framing story--trying to minimize the technological signature of the last human colony world against a superior, but technologically frozen alien race.   <span id="freeText16749901001515828916" style="display:none">Excellent world with a wonderful problem &amp; plenty of sea action. <div id="review_101706797" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101706797" />
    <a title="Brittany" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92397-brittany"><img alt="Brittany" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1191511794p2/92397.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101706797?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brittany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brittany" href="/user/show/92397-brittany">Brittany</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92397-brittany?shelf=sciencefiction">sciencefiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101706797" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9450887025780039376">This book has gotten very good reviews from people well-versed in the field of science fiction. I'm getting back into science fiction after having been gone for a decade or so, and I forgot something very important about the field: Sometimes SF fans will rave over a book for the quality of its <i>ideas</i>, even if the writing isn't very good. I think that's the case here. The premise of this book (an uber-religious lifeboat population of human established on a planet far, far away to protect it from h</span>
  <span id="freeText9450887025780039376" style="display:none">This book has gotten very good reviews from people well-versed in the field of science fiction. I'm getting back into science fiction after having been gone for a decade or so, and I forgot something very important about the field: Sometimes SF fans will rave over a book for the quality of its <i>ideas</i>, even if the writing isn't very good. I think that's the case here. The premise of this book (an uber-religious lifeboat population of human established on a planet far, far away to protect it from humanity's tech-savvy destroyers) is absolutely fascinating. However, in execution, this book fell short. <br /><br />First of all, the protagonist is the sort of beautiful, wise, funny, self-sacrificing character that gets old very fast if s/he isn't Lymond. Of course, Weber has an explanation for this and he <i>almost</i> pulls it off, but every now and then Nimue/Merlin (and I'm sorry, but those name choices really irritated the heck out of me) slips from being interesting to being grating and annoying. <br /><br />Second, the world-building was very interesting but extremely confusing, and not very complete. Much was left out that I would have been interested to learn, if Weber was going that direction. I would argue, though, that we didn't need nearly that degree of exposition and that the story would have played better without it. <br /><br />Thirdly, there were just WAY too many characters and rulers for me to keep straight. Again, I know this is a trait of the sub-genre and I used to love it, but anymore I just feel like the author should have done a better job drawing fewer characters. <br /><br />Finally, the whole thing is too long! Now, I don't want you to think I'm a wuss about long books. Actually, I love them. The fatter the book and the longer the series, the better. As long as there's enough story to support it, which I felt like there wasn't here. It took an awesome work of will for me to finish this book (I <i>did</i> want to know how this was all going to end up) only to figure out that it was the first in the series. I sincerely doubt I will be reading the rest. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9450887025780039376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780765353979" href="/book/show/1348145.Off_Armageddon_Reef">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5049442-soo?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2537103977" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12353664676403365466">Mini-Review:<br/><br/>4 Stars for Audiobook, 3.5 Stars for Story<br/><br/>4 Stars for Great Narration by Oliver Wyman<br/>4 Stars for Characters (Fun Mix)<br/>3 Stars for Foundation Background/Religion/Current Society<br/>3 Stars for Chunky Progression<br/>3.5 Stars for Battles <br/><br/>The book was written in 2007 but the phrasing definitely feels like an older piece that could have come from the 80's. I had to double check the published date because I could have sworn it was before 2000. <br/><br/>Overall, I enjoyed the story. It felt like one of thos</span>
  <span id="freeText12353664676403365466" style="display:none">Mini-Review:<br /><br />4 Stars for Audiobook, 3.5 Stars for Story<br /><br />4 Stars for Great Narration by Oliver Wyman<br />4 Stars for Characters (Fun Mix)<br />3 Stars for Foundation Background/Religion/Current Society<br />3 Stars for Chunky Progression<br />3.5 Stars for Battles <br /><br />The book was written in 2007 but the phrasing definitely feels like an older piece that could have come from the 80's. I had to double check the published date because I could have sworn it was before 2000. <br /><br />Overall, I enjoyed the story. It felt like one of those old epic fantasy tales with daring heroes. The setting and characters are drawn in clean lines of good and bad. There's no guessing on who is on what side. The broad outlines of the plot are simple but the details that make them come to life were varied and layered. The characters were the strong point for sure. <br /><br />I wanted more depth to the plot/characters/etc but realized that this isn't that kind of story. It's more of a what if game with a handful of conditions. All of the elements have been configured and tossed into the ring. I can sit back, enjoy the ride and see how it collides. I'm okay with that. <br /><br />Sometimes, I read the book blurb to re-check names/etc while I write the review. I'm glad I didn't read it before reading the story because it basically summarizes the whole plot for Off Armageddon Reef. LOL </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781429920575" href="/book/show/8110484-off-armageddon-reef">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15703983-patremagne?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15703983-patremagne?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer732118419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7717298502872526856">The last 100 pages were unbelievable. Book was a bit long, though.<br/>-------------------------------<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://abitterdraft.com/2013/10/off-armageddon-reef-by-david-weber.html'>http://abitterdraft.com/2013/10/off-a...</a><br/><br/><br/>Picture this: an incredibly powerful race of aliens known as the Gdaba halted the human exploration in space and all but wiped humanity out. The remainder of the human fleet splits up – one half cloaks and stops moving, the other continues flying to draw the Gdaba away. The half of the fleet that cloaked and escaped colonized an Earth-like planet called Safehol</span>
  <span id="freeText7717298502872526856" style="display:none">The last 100 pages were unbelievable. Book was a bit long, though.<br />-------------------------------<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://abitterdraft.com/2013/10/off-armageddon-reef-by-david-weber.html">http://abitterdraft.com/2013/10/off-a...</a><br /><br /><br />Picture this: an incredibly powerful race of aliens known as the Gdaba halted the human exploration in space and all but wiped humanity out. The remainder of the human fleet splits up – one half cloaks and stops moving, the other continues flying to draw the Gdaba away. The half of the fleet that cloaked and escaped colonized an Earth-like planet called Safehold.<br /><br />Here’s the catch – the Gdaba can detect any technology of the industrial level or higher. Humanity is forced to revert to a medieval society, where Safeholdians know nothing of their space-faring past and all believe in one religion run by a very strict church. Nimue Alban’s mind awakes in the body of an android and she is given the task of helping Safehold break away from its medieval prison, despite the teachings of the oppressive church, and eventually take on the Gdaba. Since Safehold is a medieval society, Nimue’s android body is made male, and she takes on the pseudonym Merlin Athrawes. Merlin ventures to the Kingdom of Charis, an island much like Old Earth’s England, to kickstart this scientific revolution.<br /><br /><br />I’ve never read Weber’s Honor Harrington series, but I’ve heard nothing but praise for the man’s talent. The premise for Safehold seemed awesome, and with Weber’s credentials I couldn’t imagine being disappointed.<br /><br />I noticed a few things right off the bat that I found questionable, the first being the unconventional names. I’m all for having fantasy-like names such as Tisamon or Bayaz as opposed to modern names like Jim or Tom. Weber decided utilize the fact that humanity had to restart in picking names, so we’re left with ones like Haarahld instead of Harold and Bynzhamin rather than Benjamin. The idea makes sense and it’s kind of cool when you think of how names evolved, but on paper and with a cast as large as Safehold‘s, it’s a bit of a struggle to remember them (though this is a nonfactor with the audiobook). They’re definitely something you get used to the more you read, and you have plenty of time to get used to them with 800 pages in the paperback, which leads me to my next point.<br /><br />The Honor Harrington books were rather average in length, floating between 350 and 450 pages for the most part. Safehold books are more or less all upwards of 700 with some even pushing close to 1,000 pages. In most reviews of the series you’ll find that people comment on Weber’s infodumps. While trudging through Off Armageddon Reef‘s 800 pages, the infodumps are readily apparent, and they really mess with the pacing. Occasional bits of information are nice, especially where background is needed. It seemed like Weber was almost flaunting, though flaunting is too harsh a word, his being a font of knowledge, for dozens and dozens of pages at a time. The book could have been slimmed by several hundreds of pages and been truly outstanding.<br /><br />I bet an eyebrow or two were raised at the mention of Nimue taking the form of a man to make acquiring a position of influence easier, especially with Weber’s ability to write strong female characters. While Merlin is a man, Nimue’s female mind is definitely still inside the android. She doesn’t just magically lose her attraction to men, which leads to some hilariously awkward situations. Like I said, the cast of characters is huge, full of bastards like Prince Nahrmahn of Emerald and people you ‘t help but love like King Haarahld and Prince Cayleb of Charis.<br /><br />Weber’s infodumps are sometimes very useful and necessary, but often extremely slow the pace of the tome that is Off Armageddon Reef. The final 150 or so pages made everything worthwhile, though, as they include some of the most outstanding depictions of naval combat I have ever read. Off Armageddon Reef, while overlong, was definitely worth the read, and while I’d like nothing more than to avoid getting caught up in a series that may very well exceed ten books of over 800 pages each, I will definitely be attempting to catch up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7717298502872526856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_137493052" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137493052" />
    <a title="Erica Anderson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3676259-erica-anderson"><img alt="Erica Anderson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442486349p2/3676259.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137493052?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erica Anderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erica Anderson" href="/user/show/3676259-erica-anderson">Erica Anderson</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3676259-erica-anderson?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3676259-erica-anderson?shelf=dnf">dnf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3676259-erica-anderson?shelf=2010-reads">2010-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137493052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1116383865197851020">I'm a fan of Weber's Honor Harrington books, and as I read the prologue to <i>Armageddon</i>, I settled in with a smile, anticipating some heart-pounding space battles in which clever strategy and human determination would eventually vanquish the foul Gbaba foe. <br/><br/>What I got was unexpected--a plot set dirt-side amidst a complex theocracy peopled by so many characters that I couldn't keep track of them. About a third of the way through, I realized that I was forcing myself to keep reading--something I've </span>
  <span id="freeText1116383865197851020" style="display:none">I'm a fan of Weber's Honor Harrington books, and as I read the prologue to <i>Armageddon</i>, I settled in with a smile, anticipating some heart-pounding space battles in which clever strategy and human determination would eventually vanquish the foul Gbaba foe. <br /><br />What I got was unexpected--a plot set dirt-side amidst a complex theocracy peopled by so many characters that I couldn't keep track of them. About a third of the way through, I realized that I was forcing myself to keep reading--something I've never had to do with a Weber book before.<br /><br />Though I enjoyed the premise and those characters that I could keep straight, I gave up when I realized that this wasn't the sort of military SF that I'd anticipated. I wouldn't characterize this book as fantasy, but it isn't really SF either. It's a genre-bending mix of alternate reality world-building. Let me be clear that although I was disappointed this wasn't the beginning of a new military SF series (is it?), it was the slow pacing and the rapid introduction of way too many characters that caused me to put this book down. I have to agree with other readers that some heavier editing would have been helpful, though I also understand that authors need to pack a lot into the first book of a new series.<br /><br />I'm glad that I still have a bunch of Harrington books to read. I'll have to wait and see whether I dive back into the Safehold series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1116383865197851020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_261424990" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/261424990" />
    <a title="Kathy Davie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4222001-kathy-davie"><img alt="Kathy Davie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285089902p2/4222001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/261424990?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathy Davie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathy Davie" href="/user/show/4222001-kathy-davie">Kathy Davie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222001-kathy-davie?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222001-kathy-davie?shelf=military">military</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222001-kathy-davie?shelf=action">action</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer261424990" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3920439551755733543">First in the Safehold military science fiction series revolving around a god-like being and the path he prefers for what remains of mankind.<br/><br/><b>My Take</b><br/>Personally, I give it a "7".<br/><br/>Oh, wow!! Damn it, it's a David Weber. I simply could not put it down once I got into it. Weber really knows how to create a cast of characters you cheer on and boo. For those of you who enjoy Bernard Cornwell's Richard Sharpe series or naval histories like Patrick O'Brian's Aubrey/Maturin series, you will pick this up <em>now</em>!</span>
  <span id="freeText3920439551755733543" style="display:none">First in the Safehold military science fiction series revolving around a god-like being and the path he prefers for what remains of mankind.<br /><br /><b>My Take</b><br />Personally, I give it a "7".<br /><br />Oh, wow!! Damn it, it's a David Weber. I simply could not put it down once I got into it. Weber really knows how to create a cast of characters you cheer on and boo. For those of you who enjoy Bernard Cornwell's Richard Sharpe series or naval histories like Patrick O'Brian's Aubrey/Maturin series, you will pick this up <em>now</em>!<br /><br />It began as a difference of opinion over how man was safest: evolving or remaining static. What it has become is a very corrupt society in which priests take bribes or pay them out to get the results that benefit the individual priest or the upper ranks of the priesthood.<br /><br />The names crack me up. Weber took normal names and then substituted "y"s for "a"s, "i"s, "o"s, and "u"s at times. Check 'em out. Read it quickly and you'll find the standard name we're accustomed to, lying within. Toss in the odd "h" and it really throws things off. Okay, I do appreciate the effort to accomplish this, and it does add to the atmosphere, but personally, I'd rather have the normal name as it would make it easier to keep track of who's who. And I wouldn't spend so much time stumbling over the odd spellings as I read! I am curious as to the <em>why</em> of "Nimue" and "Merlin". I rather expected the primary good guy to be named "Arthur"! (We do get an Excalibur!)<br /><br /><em>Okay, here's an e-reader function I want…a way to replace names in the story on your own personal version…!</em><br /><br />If you are particularly religious and believe in the sanctity of Church, don't read this. You'll hate it. If you acknowledge that a Church is made up of fallible men and can handle most of the Church being presented as the bad guy, you'll at least enjoy the story. I'm agnostic so…<br /><br />Fortunately, Weber provides a quick synopsis of the Books of the bible as written by the different archangels of Langhorne and Bédard's side with most of them practical guides to life, although it doesn't appear until late in the story.<br /><br />The push behind all the innovation in Charis is a result of the Church's political machinations and envy but the best part of the story is the progress we are involved in with the improvements in foundries, shipbuilding, designing sail layouts, the introduction of the abacus — can you imagine trying to add and subtract using Roman numerals!?!?! Arghhhh!!! Merlin introduces other hand-operated machines as well.<br /><br />It's a very busy story with all but two countries actively against Charis. Thanks to Merlin's SNARCs, we get to listen in on the plotting and planning of Charis' enemies. Do not however think that Weber doesn't throw in the tension. <em>Hah!</em> My heart was in my mouth and my eyes were racing across the pages desperate to know what would happen next. And Weber doesn't make it easy. There are plenty of action sequences that leave you wondering in fear if this character or that will survive. There isn't any romance; the closest is the mention of a couple of wives (I suspect that Nahrmahn's daughter Princess Mahrya (Moira??) will end up betrothed to Cayleb in a future installment) and some issues Merlin has with body reactions…well, hey, it's still the female Nimue underneath that male sculpting, and she is definitely reacting to all those nude men playing rugby in the water!<br /><br />Oh, I do love Cayleb's words to Thirsk after the battle at Crag Reach!!<br /><br /><b>The Story</b><br />A species humans named the Gbaba are intent on erasing mankind from existence, and humanity is damned if they'll go gently. There are not many humans left, and they have concocted an elaborate ruse to gain mankind time to reestablish themselves on a planet as far away beyond possible from any Gbaba detection. Using what they've learned from past Gbaba destructive raids, they design a new past for man. One in which there is no technology beyond muscle, no desire to innovate.<br /><br />The dividing factor was Langhorne, Bédard and their clique's choosing to make themselves out as archangels who were revered as messengers of the god and who refused to permit mankind to ever realize what they had managed to achieve. Unfortunately for Pei Kau-yung, Langhorne and his won and Pei Shan-wei and her colony were wiped off the planet Safehold and the "bible" was re-written to showcase Shan-wei as evil.<br /><br />Over 800 years later, the AI secreted by Pei Kau-yung, Pei Shan-wei, and their sect revives a PICA, a Personality-Integrated Cybernetic Avatar, using Nimue's personality. A recording by Pei explains the founding and history of why Safehold was established as well as the reasons for the infighting. The PICA's mission is to decide if she should interfere with mankind's current existence. An existence in which the Temple of God, Mother Church, rules the entire planet. Enough time has passed, 890 years, that corruption has settled deeply into the Church as they manipulate kings and countries to the Church's benefit.<br /><br />Today they are threatened by Haarald VII of Charis whose father established a Royal College dedicated to finding new ways to combine current knowledge to enhance efficiency — terrifying, for it promotes change. Charis is far enough away from the Temple of God in Zion that there is less Church oversight. It's not really the Charisians' innovations that are the problem. It's more that their Navy and their merchant fleet makes Charis a powerful nation. A threat to Mother Church's autonomy. A threat that builds up the Church's fears and sets change in motion.<br /><br />Events are already in motion in Charis for Merlin Athrawes, a.k.a., the PICA Nimue, erupts into their lives when he saves Cayleb from an assassination attempt. Bringing with him all the knowledge and his interface abilities with OWL (Ordoñes-Westinghouse-Lytton RAPIER tactical computer) which will aid the Charisians in their coming fight against those who envy them.<br /><br /><b>The Characters</b><br />There is a cast of thousands, so I'll only relate the more important names.<br /><br /><strong>Merlin Athrawes</strong> (?) is the male version of Nimue the PICA. Quite conveniently, the PICA can affect minor physical changes and since Safehold is male-oriented, Nimue has chosen a male physique. <strong>Cayleb</strong> is the Crown Prince of Charis. Young, but brought up in his forefathers' shadow of kindness, decency, and responsibility to his people. <strong>Ahrnahld Falkhan</strong> (Arnold Falcon), a Charisian Marine, is in charge of the prince's security.<br /><br /><strong>King Haarahld VII (Harold) of Charis</strong> is Cayleb's father — <em>you'll absolutely love him and wish more rulers could be like him.</em> <strong>Rayjhis Yowance</strong> (Regis ??), Earl of Gray Harbor, is his first councillor. <strong>Bynzhamyn Raice </strong>(Benjamin Race), Baron Wave Thunder, is his spymaster. Sir Rhyzhard Seafarmer (Richard) is Wave Thunder's second-in-command. <strong>Kahlvyn Ahrmahk</strong> (Calvin Armac??), Duke of Tirian, is Gray Harbor's beloved son-in-law, raised as a brother with the king and Haarahld's staunch supporter, fourth in the succession, and a much-loved cousin of Cayleb's. And a traitor. <strong>Bishop Maikel Staynair</strong> (Michael) is a Charisian and appointed by the king much to Mother Church's dismay; he is also an honest man with a Christian interpretation of <em>Writ</em>. <em>Um, the original definition of "Christian".</em><br /><br /><strong>High Admiral Bryahn Lock Island</strong> (Brian), Earl of Lock Island, commands key fortifications at Tellesberg's harbor mouth in Charis. <strong>Doctor Rahzhyr Mahklyn</strong> (Roger Maclin?) is the Dean of the Royal College of Charis. <strong>Ehdwyrd Howsmyn</strong> (Edward Houseman) "owns two of the kingdom's three largest foundries and one of the largest shipyards and a small fleet of merchant ships". <strong>Raiyan Mychail</strong> (Ryan Michael) is the Royal Navy's primary sailmaker. <strong>Sir Dustyn Olyvyr </strong>(Dustin Oliver) is one of the top ship designers and the "chief naval constructor of the Royal Charisian Navy".<strong> Sir Ahlfryd Hyndryk </strong>(Alfred Hendrick), Baron Seamount, is a Navy captain and <em>the</em> gunnery expert of Safehold.<br /><br /><em>Enemies of Charis</em><br /><strong>Oskahr Mhulvayn </strong>(Oscar Mulvayne?) is a spy for Prince Hektor in Tellesberg. His boss is <strong>Zhaspahr Maysahn</strong> (Jasper Mason). <strong>Zhames Makferzahn</strong> (James MacPherson) replaces Mhulvayn after the purge. Communication is through <strong>Captain Whaite</strong> (Wait? White?), a.k.a., Lieutenant Robyrt Bradlai (Robert Bradley) of the League Navy. <strong>Braidee Lahang</strong> (Brady Lang) is another spy.<br /><br /><strong>Prince Nahrmahn (Norman) of Emerald</strong> is a greedy jerk without a longterm view; he spies and plots against Charis. <strong>Baron Hahl Shandyr</strong> (Hal Sander?) is his spymaster. <strong>Trahvys Ohlsyn</strong> (Travis Olson), Earl of Pine Hollow, is his chief councillor while <strong>Gharth Rahlstahn</strong> (Garth Ralston), Earl of Mahndyr, is the commander of the Emerald Navy.<br /><br /><strong>Prince Hektor of Corisande</strong> hates the Charisians. <strong>Phylyp Ahzgood</strong> (Phillip Osgood) is his spymaster. <strong>King Rahnyld (Ronald) of Dohlar</strong>. <strong>Samyl Cahkrayn</strong> (Samuel Cochrane), Duke of Fern, is Rahnyld's chief councillor. <strong>Admiral Lywys Gardynyr</strong> (Lewis Gardener), Earl of Thirsk, is second-in-command of the Dohlar Navy sent out to ravage Charis. Good thing he's not in charge! That honor goes to the very incompetent <strong>Admiral General Duke Malikai</strong> (gads, you can almost tell how incompetent he is by the number of titles!). <strong>King Gorjah of Tarot</strong> (?) has an alliance with Charis against which he chafes. <strong><br /><br />Queen Sharleyan of Chisholm</strong> is one of the few rulers very reluctant to war against Charis. <strong>Mahrak Sandyrs </strong>(Mark Sanders), <strong>Baron Green Mountain</strong>, is First Councillor in Chisholm. <strong>Sir Lewk Cohlmyn</strong> (Luke Colman) is the Earl of Sharpfield and the senior admiral for the Chisholm Navy. Together, they come up with their best plan to "comply" with Mother Church with the least damage to Charis.<br /> <br /><em>The Church</em><br /><strong>Father Paityr Wylsynn</strong> (Peter Wilson) is the intendant (inquisitor) in Charis; the Church's problem is that he's honest. <strong>Erayk Dynnys</strong> (Eric Dennis) is the Archbishop of Charis spending the majority of his year in Zion and a month in Tellesberg. <strong>Bishop Executor Zherald Ahdymsyn</strong> (A French "Gerald"?? Adamson) is Dynnys' representative in Charis year-round. <strong>Father Symyn</strong> (Simon) is Dynnys' new secretary — and a zealous spy for Clyntahn.<br /><br />There are four priests who form a <strong>Council of Vicars</strong>, the Church's rulers: <strong>Zhaspyr Clyntahn</strong> (Jasper Clinton) is the Grand Inquisitor as well as a glutton and has a major hate on against Father Paityr and his family; <strong>Zahsyn Trynair</strong> (Jason Trainer??) is the true power of the Council, the Chancellor; <strong>Rhobair Duchairn</strong> (a French "Robert"? ??), the Treasurer General; and, <strong>Allayn Magwair</strong> (Allan ??) is the Captain General. <strong>Erek XVII</strong> is the Grand Vicar, but a figurehead (it's said that Erek "demonstrates his independence by choosing which pair of shoes he would wear")<br /><br /><strong>"Archangels" Langhorne</strong> and <strong>Bédard</strong> prevailed and created a society which follows the Langhorne Bible religiously and forbids any innovations that may create emissions.<br /><br /><b>The Cover and Title</b><br />The cover is split horizontally in three sections with a deep gunmetal gray as top and bottom containing the author's name and the title while the middle band is a yellow-bordered window into the world of Safehold with its castle-like buildings with a space skimmer flying through its sky.<br /><br />The title refers to a reef created by the winners in a philosophical fight over man's evolution when they obliterated the city located in Armageddon, specifically it refers to a sea battle lost <em>Off Armageddon Reef</em>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3920439551755733543" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1319941880" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1319941880" />
    <a title="Scott Holstad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9098678-scott-holstad"><img alt="Scott Holstad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505041090p2/9098678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1319941880?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Holstad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Holstad" href="/user/show/9098678-scott-holstad">Scott Holstad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9098678-scott-holstad?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9098678-scott-holstad?shelf=david-weber">david-weber</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1319941880" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15635385521108856649">This is a really good book starting what is probably a really good series, yet it has some problems. First, it is about a world called Safehold, humanity's last planet, because an alien race that can detect technology has wiped out the rest of humanity. As a result, this planet was colonized by people who were mind wiped and given medieval technology and given a theocratic rule, by the Church of God Awaiting, founded by "Archangels," who are the people who founded the colony. Nine hundred years </span>
  <span id="freeText15635385521108856649" style="display:none">This is a really good book starting what is probably a really good series, yet it has some problems. First, it is about a world called Safehold, humanity's last planet, because an alien race that can detect technology has wiped out the rest of humanity. As a result, this planet was colonized by people who were mind wiped and given medieval technology and given a theocratic rule, by the Church of God Awaiting, founded by "Archangels," who are the people who founded the colony. Nine hundred years later, an AI "wakes" up in a cave to discover she/he has been lying dormant, waiting for the right time to emerge and wake humanity up and bring technology back to the people. The Church, which rules all, is completely corrupt. Merlin, the indestructible AI, goes to the kingdom of Charis, which he feels is most likely to accept change. He saves the prince from an assassination attempt, and then the first councilor from another assassination attempt, and is given a spot in the king's court. <br /><br />And so it begins. The Church decides it no longer likes Charis. Merlin has been helping make changes in Charis, introducing looms, sails, muskets, quick firing cannons, etc., and the Church forces five neighboring kingdoms to gang up on Charis and sail in their navies to destroy the kingdom. There are a couple of major naval battles, which are written pretty well, and the book ends. At about 800 pages. It's a long book. And it could have done with an editor. It's too long.<br /><br />Some of the problems. There are far too many characters. Weber loves characters. He loves to introduce them in his Honor Harrington series, but this is ridiculous! There must be hundreds of them here, and they all have titles -- Sir, Earl, Duke, Prince, King, Vicar, etc. And to make matters worse, he thinks he's being clever by intentionally misspelling them. Instead of spelling common names normally, he adds vowels and consonants, so that the names look like Haarahld, Nahrmahn, Maysahn, Kahlvyn, and Makferzahn. It's freaking stupid and it makes it totally impossible to remember them all. Impossible. Did I say stupid? Also, if I have to see the phrase, "He bared his teeth" one more time, I'm going to puke! Weber -- people don't bare their damn teeth!!! Dogs do. Wolves do. People don't. And he wrote this at least 15 times. It was damned annoying. And did I say the book was too long? Talk about infodumps! He could have pruned this book by 200 pages and still made it good. Was he being paid by the word?<br /><br />Irritants aside, it was a pretty good book. He handles separation of church and state pretty well. The naval battles are really well done. The heroes are heroic and the bad guys are pure evil. Merlin is a little two dimensional, but he kicks ass and that's cool. I've already started on the next book, so I obviously wanted to know what happened next. This isn't exactly sci fi. It's partially fantasy, I guess? World building? Whatever the case, it's recommended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15635385521108856649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nooilforpacifists" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nooilforpacifists" href="/user/show/26071992-nooilforpacifists">Nooilforpacifists</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3274857551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4844680797604002327">What a great notion—a space opera deliberately tuned to fans of Napoleonic naval stories. I’m a geek of both genres, so I looked forward to this series. And it was fun to recreate—with sailing ships, on an Earth’s daughter deliberately denied technology—the great naval battles of history. The reader is treated to at least the Battle of Cape St. Vincent; Trafalgar; French overwhelming the British off the Virginia coast to end the Revolutionary War, and perhaps others I missed. Some jolly good fun</span>
  <span id="freeText4844680797604002327" style="display:none">What a great notion—a space opera deliberately tuned to fans of Napoleonic naval stories. I’m a geek of both genres, so I looked forward to this series. And it was fun to recreate—with sailing ships, on an Earth’s daughter deliberately denied technology—the great naval battles of history. The reader is treated to at least the Battle of Cape St. Vincent; Trafalgar; French overwhelming the British off the Virginia coast to end the Revolutionary War, and perhaps others I missed. Some jolly good fun.<br /><br />But this is David Weber writing. One imagines his house as a tesseract of rooms, each with a different set of characters, maps, plot lines, pinned up on the wall, so Weber doesn’t mix books. Except for the “Honorverse,” Weber punches out a great first book, then s l o w s the pace so that two novels should be three, or three two. I found myself skimming quite a bit—not just for the next battle; we all know Weber puts one at the end—but for the next bit of information interesting enough to read before the battle.<br /><br />I quit after book four. The first was a good four stars; second one three stars; number two gets two stars—well you see where this geometric progression is heading. <br /><br /><br />I’m afraid were I to keep reading books in the series, my ratings would be:<br /> ____<br />\/ -1</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1423702392" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1423702392" />
    <a title="Rindis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/145461-rindis"><img alt="Rindis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182468953p2/145461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1423702392?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rindis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rindis" href="/user/show/145461-rindis">Rindis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6495720-off-armageddon-reef">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Rindis by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4739221-m-c-a-hogarth">M.C.A. Hogarth</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/145461-rindis?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1423702392" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16477278152178285263">David Weber is a good author with a few glaring weaknesses. Sadly, all of that is readily apparent in this novel.<br/><br/>The basic setup is that humanity gets to the stars, runs into an alien race apparently intent on wiping out everything other than itself, and after a tough war, looses. Earth, and all of the colonies, are destroyed, leaving one fleet with a last-ditch plan to start a new colony somewhere out of the way, avoid technology, and tell-tale high-energy emissions that will give the new colon</span>
  <span id="freeText16477278152178285263" style="display:none">David Weber is a good author with a few glaring weaknesses. Sadly, all of that is readily apparent in this novel.<br /><br />The basic setup is that humanity gets to the stars, runs into an alien race apparently intent on wiping out everything other than itself, and after a tough war, looses. Earth, and all of the colonies, are destroyed, leaving one fleet with a last-ditch plan to start a new colony somewhere out of the way, avoid technology, and tell-tale high-energy emissions that will give the new colony away.<br /><br />The prologue for all of this is excellently done, but is big and detailed enough that you'd think the point of the series is the buildup of Safehold to where it can try for a round two against the aliens.<br /><br />Apparently not, it's all set up for a long series of novels with lots of Age of Sail-style action. Not necessarily a bad thing, and let's be honest, it's Weber's first love. But after all the initial high-tech setup, and the fact that that is the supposed end goal, I was hoping for a thick novel that deals with, shall we say, an inflection point in Safehold's history, and then the next one could be a couple generations later, and so on, back to actually revisiting that prologue.<br /><br />Inside of what we <em>do</em> have here, we Merlin, who's basically Superman. As a high-tech android with the personality of a dead Earth naval officer, he's got everything you can ask for: super strength, lightning reflexes, a library full of banned scientific knowledge... and a lack of allies. Actually, he does find those, and of course, war and action result.<br /><br />The world building that surrounds this is excellent. That's always been one of Weber's strengths, and it is on display here, and is one of the primary reasons to read this. The plot is fairly strong as well, though it's not really an 800-page plot; the book doesn't need as much trimming as some other reviews say, but it <em>does</em> need some overall tightening up. If you want some lower-tech space opera, definitely read this, and I certainly plan on going on with the series eventually, but I can't give it much more than a weak recommendation for particular audiences.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16477278152178285263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1423702392">

<div id="review_1703262586" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1703262586" />
    <a title="Joe Santoro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6676200-joe-santoro"><img alt="Joe Santoro" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1703262586?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe Santoro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe Santoro" href="/user/show/6676200-joe-santoro">Joe Santoro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6676200-joe-santoro?shelf=hard_sf">hard_sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6676200-joe-santoro?shelf=naval_fiction">naval_fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1703262586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7479191794278342901">I had a hard time rating this book... I wavered between 2 and 5 stars at various times. To some extent, there's a bit of a bait and switch.. the description and the first 50 pages or so are an alien invasion story. One could picture it taking place in the distant past of the Honor Harrington universe (which is alluded to but never discussed). Sadly for the book's characters, the aliens are overwhelming and unwilling to communicate, so they resort to sending 1/2 the last fleet secrets thousands o</span>
  <span id="freeText7479191794278342901" style="display:none">I had a hard time rating this book... I wavered between 2 and 5 stars at various times. To some extent, there's a bit of a bait and switch.. the description and the first 50 pages or so are an alien invasion story. One could picture it taking place in the distant past of the Honor Harrington universe (which is alluded to but never discussed). Sadly for the book's characters, the aliens are overwhelming and unwilling to communicate, so they resort to sending 1/2 the last fleet secrets thousands of lightyears away to start anew.<br /><br />They plan to not use any technological emissions until they can outpace the aliens, but some of the survivors instead set themselves up as a new religion, which is based around not using technology involving emissions (electricity, radio, etc). Some disagree, and they fight, but the god makers win.<br /><br />Fast foward 800 years, and society is quite settled with their made up religion, when an android of one of the technological people wakes up, and decides to try to take the society forward to fight the aliens some day.<br /><br />All that sounds really interesting, but, sadly, there's no other mention of the alien threat... I suspect ever again in the series (I poked through a later book in the series, and only a few years pass). What the book REALLY is, is a 'tech development' type series(like Ring of Fire or Destroyermen). The 'good guys', Charis, are, much like Manticore, an idealized British analogue with an enlightened Royal family and smart, innovative, plucky citizens. <br /><br />The Church of God Awaiting (the religion that got made up way back to keep technology down), has developed in 750 years into an analogue of the worst corrupted version the dark ages Christian church, just before the reformation. The book essentially becomes 'what if Henry the VIII had spy satelittes and better cannons than anyone else'.. which is actually quite fun and well done. It's just not at all what the book advertised.<br /><br />There were also a few very annoying tics that served to repeatedly through you out of the story . . All the names are 'regular' ones that are spelled in a strange, phonetic way (like Zhan for John... Zhysstyn for Justin, Norhmahn for Norman, etc). I get he's trying to show the drift of the language, but it gets annoying really fast.<br /><br />There's also a few times where he forgets it's not a modern story. The religion has one woman named 'Shen-Wei' as the leader of the resistance to the original gods.. she essentially becomes the devil, and her name is used in place of 'hell' and 'damn'... except when Weber forgets. He also uses a few other modern expressions that make no sense in context.<br /><br />Worse, though is the cuteness. For some reason, baseball gets into the religious rules, and Weber uses real players (with annoying spelled versions of their names) in the game, which seems to serve no purpose other than to be a shout out to the Atlanta Braves. Then there's a valet named Jeeves.. a couple others that I don't recall.<br /><br />Overall, though, I was exciting and interested to see what happened in the end, and I'm excited to read the next one, so that definitely qualifies as a good book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7479191794278342901" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_768305615" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/768305615" />
    <a title="Aildiin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8639071-aildiin"><img alt="Aildiin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363194138p2/8639071.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/768305615?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aildiin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aildiin" href="/user/show/8639071-aildiin">Aildiin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6495720-off-armageddon-reef">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8639071-aildiin?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8639071-aildiin?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer768305615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4945395545685122905">I have been a long time fan of David Weber but for some weird reason I had stayed away from his Safehold universe. I was wrong.<br/>This is one of the best book David Weber has written in a while and I am very glad I finally decided to delve in it.<br/>The premises are the following :<br/>While venturing through the stars humanity encountered an aggressive Alien race (the Gbaba) bent on destroying any race it met. After a long fight humanity lost but managed to establish one last stealth remote colony before t</span>
  <span id="freeText4945395545685122905" style="display:none">I have been a long time fan of David Weber but for some weird reason I had stayed away from his Safehold universe. I was wrong.<br />This is one of the best book David Weber has written in a while and I am very glad I finally decided to delve in it.<br />The premises are the following :<br />While venturing through the stars humanity encountered an aggressive Alien race (the Gbaba) bent on destroying any race it met. After a long fight humanity lost but managed to establish one last stealth remote colony before the end. This colony is located at the other side of the Galaxy on a world called Safehold. The colony was established with the premises that one day it would grow and avenge humanity and beat the Gbaba but it was perverted from its foundation. Instead the original colonists were indoctrinated, all memory from the past was wiped from them and they were made to believe that technology was bad to prevent them from ever developing technology advanced enough that could be detected from the stars ( so no radio signals). This came at the cost of civil war between the original colonist planners and overseers which resulted in them wiping each others.<br /><br />800 years later an AI imprinted with the brain of the daughter of the original military commander of the colony wakes up ( he was one of the good guys). The world is a medieval one ruled with an iron fist by a corrupt church bent on enforcing the original edict of a stagnant technology base. In memory of its father and of what humanity really was, it will do everything to throw away the yoke of the church and help humanity pursue its original mission.<br /><br />That's it for the summary.<br />For the book itself itself we see one realm fights against its enemies supported by the church in a setting very similar to early renaissance.<br />The character building isn't exceptional but it's decent, and there is a huge emphasis on naval battle in the second half of the book, in a way that is very characteristic of David Weber. One side is heavily outnumbered but has a heavy technological advantage.<br />I have to add a few things before I conclude this review however.<br />Based on me browsing through the later books in the series it seems obvious to me too that we will never see humanity fighting the Gbaba, the whole series is about freeing the planet ( the 7th book is coming soon and it still hasn't happened).<br />Still I have to recommend this and I will be reading the next book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4945395545685122905" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_178908310" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178908310" />
    <a title="***Dave Hill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4631805-dave-hill"><img alt="***Dave Hill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605886180p2/4631805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178908310?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="***Dave Hill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="***Dave Hill" href="/user/show/4631805-dave-hill">***Dave Hill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4631805-dave-hill?shelf=text">text</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178908310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4682211870049883067">There's a lot to like here for David Weber fans. It's a new series for one thing, and (aside from some brief introductory chapters) there's no space opera.<br/><br/>There's sea opera, instead.<br/><br/>There are classic Weber themes here -- multiple nations vying against each other, the advantages that technological advances give, massive battles, tragic and noble deaths, incompetent commanders leading to military disaster, the dangers of Ludditism, and the venality of churches that take on temporal power (vs. the </span>
  <span id="freeText4682211870049883067" style="display:none">There's a lot to like here for David Weber fans. It's a new series for one thing, and (aside from some brief introductory chapters) there's no space opera.<br /><br />There's sea opera, instead.<br /><br />There are classic Weber themes here -- multiple nations vying against each other, the advantages that technological advances give, massive battles, tragic and noble deaths, incompetent commanders leading to military disaster, the dangers of Ludditism, and the venality of churches that take on temporal power (vs. the virtue of believers who also believe in freedom).<br /><br />Oh, and the naval battles. Lots (eventually) of big, exciting naval battles, Battles of Lepanto writ large. It's like Weber really jonesed for some naval warfare, and set up a pretty spiffy SF/Fantasy world in which to do it.<br /><br />Ont the other hand, it's hardly without flaws. <br /><br />1. After some pretty exciting world-building, the action shifts into ... politics. Lots of politics. Lots and lots of politics, church and state, lots of nations, and bushel-baskets-full of players. It drags the action down to a screeching halt, from which it doesn't recover for about half the book.<br /><br />2. The above isn't helped by Weber's decision to show linguistic drift by turning modifying common names -- Haarahld, Kahlvyn, Nahrmahn, Makferzahn, Maysahn ... It's an intersting idea, but it fills every name with such unusual morphemes that it's highly distracting, and slows the eye at every passage so you can sound the name out.<br /><br />3. The ostensible protagonist -- Nimue/Merlin -- is something of a cypher. We get a bit of perspective early on, but for most of the book, the character varies between Brave Hero and Quiet Counsellor. But it's all action, and little inside. Merlin isn't quite two-dimensional, but he's certainly not three-dimensional. Make it 2.2-dimensional.<br /><br />There's a lot of good stuff here, and plenty of Good Guys to Root For, and Bad Guys to Hiss At (and very little doubt about Who's Who), but it also feels like it could have used a good editor to trim down the excessive sailing ship detail and verbiage. This is a 772 page book that could have been done in 500.<br /><br />I plan on picking up the next book in the series (there are three more, with a fourth coming in September) -- but I'm not so eager that I wanted to plow through to it immediately.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4682211870049883067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
